{"uri": "8260022528", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:49:57", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:49:57Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:49:11Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9764705896377563, "url": "https://journalnow.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_c8bfe970-cc5c-5d25-88dd-48a88587707a.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. Discovery Company. {"uri": "2024-08-444305722", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "09:12:21", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T09:12:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T09:00:02Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9450980424880981, "url": "https://www.cnn.com/2024/08/05/business/home-sizes-shrinking/index.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "Bigger may not always be better: According to a National Association of Homebuilders survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most US suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nA view of houses built in the city of 'Levittown,' New York, circa 1947. Real estate development company Levitt & Sons built several large suburban housing developments that came to be known as 'Levittowns' in the United States and Puerto Rico.\n\nIrving Haberman/IH Images/Getty Images/File\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"", "source": {"uri": "cnn.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "CNN"}, {"uri": "8260031001", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:55:44", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:55:44Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:54:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9333333373069763, "url": "https://scnow.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_95e238ec-c0fa-56d8-b74e-8eca7b3a8c88.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Florence 1 superintendent: Facebook post not representative of us New Lake City public charter school planned to open in August 2025 Pee Dee forecast to get flash flooding from TD 4 Driver dies from injuries suffered in July 18 I-95 crash Florence County investigators seek aid to identify person Bicyclist hurt in Florence crash with minivan Tropical weather, other storms forecast for Pee Dee Florence moves closer to a Lucas Street corridor plan Post 1 falls short in American Legion state title game Forecasters: Deadly flash floods likely from Debby's rain Post 1's late rally falls short this time as Camden holds on for win Moore Farms adds a 'practical' pavilion Cook's walk-off double lifts Post 1 into state tourney title game Former Carvers Bay star Melissa Jefferson earns bronze medal in Olympic women's 100 meters Area football teams weather the weather on 1st official fall practice\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Residential Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology Changes in Housing Inventory by State\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260083465", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "12:28:07", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T12:28:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T12:27:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.929411768913269, "url": "https://qctimes.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_25b09af4-2f9f-563b-9492-574739770445.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Review: T-Pain deserved better than what fans gave him at the Mississippi Valley Fair John Deere lays off more than 300 in Moline and East Moline in latest round of cuts Timeline: Deere has cut 2,700 jobs in 10 months in Iowa and Illinois, 1,300 in Quad-Cities UAW releases statement on John Deere layoffs, says cuts 'kill good American jobs' West Lake Okoboji home sells for state record $9.5M Review: The bests and worsts of Friday at Hinterland Music Festival Davenport man arrested in connection with robbery, shooting incident Man accused of stealing car at gunpoint in Davenport Mount Mercy and St. Ambrose to combine into single university Parents of murder victim angry with plea deal that offered reduced charge and prison sentence Gannon Hanevold: The three strangest foods we found at the Mississippi Valley Fair Five named to St. Ambrose Hall of Fame Driver of garbage truck struck by train in Scott County suffers minor injuries Prosecutors ask to dismiss cases against two men for 2023 killing in Davenport Editorial: Davenport can change trajectory with new city administrator\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Residential Housing Inventory by State Changes in Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260078987", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "12:24:41", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T12:24:41Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T12:23:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9254902005195618, "url": "https://tucson.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_2dcfa1d7-370a-5c47-9dd1-a151852e4d89.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Court reverses itself on Arizona voter registration rules Primary election day is here in Arizona. Here's what to know 100 fun events happening in Tucson this August 2024 \ud83e\uddda\ud83e\ude84 Joe Pagac has created countless murals on Tucson walls. These are his 3 favorites. Controversial Tucson-area Sen. Justine Wadsack loses primary Led by star pitcher Hattie Macumber, World Series-bound Willcox is the Little League team that could 28 FREE events happening in Tucson this August 2024 \ud83d\udcb8 Spiritual shop, new pizza joint coming to Tucson's west side Man arrested in 6-vehicle crash in Oro Valley 'It helped me a lot': Suni Lee has 'needed' heart-to-heart with teammates before Team USA won gold This new local bakery is now open with fresh bread, pies and lattes \ud83e\udd67 Ex-NBA player, Arizona Wildcat Chase Budinger wins 2024 Olympic beach volleyball debut in Paris Justine Wadsack trails Vince Leach in Tucson-area state Senate race Primary win margin shows Kari Lake must work hard to widen support, consultants say Pac-12 survival: Washington St., Oregon St. engaged in strategic discussions with multiple conferences\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Residential Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology State of the housing industry Changes in Housing Inventory by State States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260017361", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:46:20", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:46:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:45:32Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9254902005195618, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_ab5b3eab-376e-5257-a7f4-84a0b59151cc.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Sheetz gas station, convenience store planned for Marion Marion police offering free food, fun at National Night Out Sheriff: Man wanted for break-in, assault at McDowell County home West Marion speaker on McDowell's strategic planning: No Black or Brown people on committee Roses Discount Store in Marion set to close in August Sheriff: Marion man intentionally hit pedestrian with car McDowell Tech president talks food, pets and church in meeting with local officials Public meeting on 1,300-acre Burke, McDowell business site set for Aug. 6 Biscuitville named top fast-food chain for breakfast - again Stony Point woman charged with stealing $16,000 from Taylorsville church Officials: Morganton hospital's 6-story patient tower will triple size of emergency department Catawba County woman sentenced for stealing over $300,000 from elderly relatives Family of man slain at Kernersville Food Lion wants answers NC Gov. Cooper opted out of Harris VP vetting, in part over worry about GOP lieutenant, sources say Woman sues Winston-Salem police chief for defamation and 'emotional distress'\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Residential Housing Inventory by State Changes in Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. Old Pink fire was due to arson, investigators find; person of interest identified Caesars Sportsbook promo code PLAYS1000 for NFL Hall Of Fame Game: $1,000 bonus for Bears vs. Texans Camp observations: Bills run game, pass defense show out at Highmark practice Price hike: Seat prices doubling at new Bills stadium for some club season ticket holders 'Time is an enemy': Costco unlikely before 2026, Station Twelve a question mark and Boulevard Mall languishes Wegmans loses its crown as top supermarket PolitiFact: Tenney claims that Biden executive order requires federal employees to break law Alan Pergament: As of September, all three local broadcast affiliates will have 7 p.m. newscasts Jiri Kulich must 'tear up the league' with Amerks before his Sabres call comes 'A dangerous situation': Safety fixes sought after Tonawanda crash kills teen bicyclist Bills punting competition ends as team adds another safety to roster Police take shooting suspects into custody after chase over Skyway NJ developer plans overhaul of Gateway Building property in Hamburg Bills camp observations: Coach Sean McDermott concerned about number of injuries Steve Pigeon, nearing end of child sex abuse sentence, must register as sex offender for life\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260018861", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:47:10", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:47:10Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:46:24Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://missoulian.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_82da08db-a608-58fa-9cac-0b1353a1cc26.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Non-native bird, of unknown origin, roosts in Rattlesnake UPDATED: Officials ID man who died in Going-to-the-Sun Road crash 'Amazing experience': Missoula's Katharine Berkoff wins Olympic bronze in backstroke Missoula man charged after allegedly raping minor Gun store owner at center of political outcry charged with tax fraud Body of male drowning victim found in Hungry Horse Reservoir Two MSP inmates charged with kidnapping, attempted rape of officer 'Unprecedented': Missoula officials prioritize debris cleanup Missoula woman accused of homicide may seek mental health defense Montana forced to close Section 8 low-income housing voucher waitlist UPDATED: Sheriff IDs ATV driver killed in Flathead County crash U.S. women win 4x100 medley relay in world record time; Katharine Berkoff to receive gold Governor surveys Missoula after storm Fire ignites west of Lost Trail; wilderness blaze smokes Bitterroot Search continues for missing paddleboarder at Hungry Horse Reservoir\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Residential Housing Inventory by State Changes in Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Methodology\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260017103", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:46:09", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:46:09Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:45:14Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://pressofatlanticcity.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_c02f3e1c-eb58-5e25-bc90-4af4fffa76d7.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Egg Harbor Township police asking public not to camp near Spirit Halloween ahead of opening Thursday Atlantic City casino headliners for the weekend of Aug. 2 No injuries reported in Galloway Township plane crash Philadelphia man charged with murder after woman's body found on Atlantic City jetty Brigantine restricts Cove beach access due to hazardous debris Atlantic City Boardwalk medical cannabis shop to close Thousands attend Spirit Halloween's grand opening in Egg Harbor Township Hearing on receiver for Bart Blatstein's Atlantic City waterpark postponed to Aug. 16 Cape May zoo reports death of Buju the cheetah Budweiser Clydesdales coming to Margate in August Galloway Township plane crash pilot faced split-second decision These Atlantic County traffic advisories take effect Monday Note left by woman who killed Wildwood Crest native reveals abuse accusations, cancer battle Think indicted Democratic boss George Norcross only influences Camden County? Think again. Atlantic County Animal Shelter to host 'Clear the Shelters' event next month\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | Omny Studio\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Residential Housing Inventory by State Changes in Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260002666", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:37:07", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:37:07Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:36:12Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8941176533699036, "url": "https://www.nwitimes.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_47205f90-bee5-52da-b547-437992f805fb.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory State of the housing industry Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Changes in Housing Inventory by State Methodology Residential Housing Inventory by State Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros. {"uri": "8260015159", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-05", "time": "11:44:52", "dateTime": "2024-08-05T11:44:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-05T11:43:58Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.886274516582489, "url": "https://nptelegraph.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/home-gardening/new-homes-are-getting-smaller-that-could-be-big-news-for-first-time-buyers/article_e39e3f3c-2366-598a-aecc-44bbbd12c42b.html", "title": "New homes are getting smaller. That could be big news for first-time buyers", "body": "New homes continue to get smaller, despite the price tag getting bigger, and designers say it's time for the \"death of the hallway.\"\n\nAfter years of saving, Jordyn Carias and her partner purchased a home for the first time last month. To find a place that didn't break the bank, she said, they had to go smaller.\n\n\"We had a lot of things we wanted in our first home, but after looking at the price range, we had to reassess,\" Carias, a graphic designer who lives in Muscatine, Iowa, said.\n\nCompared to other countries, America is known for bigger cars, portions and take-out coffee cup sizes. For most of recent history, Americans have also wanted bigger homes -- but now that's changing.\n\nFor most of the last half-century, new single-family homes kept growing. In 1973, the median size of completed single-family homes was 1,525 square feet, according to US Census data. By 2015, that number had ballooned to 2,467 square feet.\n\nBut as the cost of buying a home has exploded and McMansions have fallen out of favor, homebuilders have reversed course, building smaller homes with an eye to first-time buyers. In 2023, the median single-family home built was 2,233 square feet, down 9% from the 2015 peak, with many formal dining rooms and \"bonus\" rooms disappearing.\n\nPeople are also reading... Shatel: Why Tony White said no to USC job, more takes from Nebraska media day APEShip Brewing opens Wednesday in Canteen District in North Platte Man changing tire struck and killed in hit-and-run near Big Springs; State Patrol seeking semi involved 7 killed after plane departing from Nebraska City crashes in Wyoming including members of gospel group Borges, Jones lead after first day of Junior Ambassador Golf Classic 'Exhausting, heartbreaking, devastating': Racism at Wayne school pushes family out of Nebraska Andrew Bottrell: While turnovers plagued the Huskers again, Rhule's rule does feel different North Platte softball faces FNBO Nationals seniors in charity softball game Nebraska Legislature reassigns Pillen's property tax plan to Revenue Committee An ode to the biggest railroad town: Canteen musical authors speak to local service groups As police kill Native Americans on this reservation, families left afraid and in the dark Who is Italian boxer Angela Carini and why did she quit her fight against Imane Khelif? Model trains, train displays and good eats as Rail Days takes over North Platte 'What would North Platte do?' Company uses Canteen story to remind employees about selflessness 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street\n\nCarias and her partner ultimately settled on a roughly 920-square-foot home. To maximize space, they decided to use the basement as their primary bedroom.\n\n\"The broader trend in larger homes had been driven by what consumers wanted and demanded,\" said Alan Ratner, a homebuilder analyst at Zelman & Associates. \"More recently, we've started to see that flip almost out of necessity, because of how challenging affordability is right now.\"\n\nHomebuyers are warming up to the idea of smaller dwellings: According to an April study from the National Association of Homebuilders, the typical buyer wants a home that is 2,067 square feet -- still smaller than the typical new home size last year.\n\nLevittowns to McMansions\n\nCarias' new home was built nearly 100 years ago. Today, smaller, often more affordable homes under 1,000 square feet are harder to come by in most suburbs.\n\nThat wasn't always the case. For a period beginning in the late-1940s, \"Levittown\" homes came to define suburban living in post-war America, Jacob Anbinder, a historian at Cornell University, said.\n\nThe housing developments, which began in the New York suburbs but spread elsewhere in the US and Puerto Rico, often fit eight 750- to 800-square foot homes on one acre.\n\n\"As many Americans became wealthier over the '40s, '50s and '60s, they wanted higher living standards and the larger homes that came with them,\" Anbinder said. \"There was almost continuous upward growth in the size of new homes.\"\n\nToday, most of the original Levittown homes no longer exist, according to Anbinder. Over the years, homeowners expanded or demolished them in favor of larger spaces.\n\nThat upward growth trend has largely tapered in recent years, though.\n\nAccording to an NAHB survey, 17% of homebuilders said they built homes on smaller lots in 2023 to support home sales, and 14% said they built more townhouses.\n\nEarlier this year, D.R. Horton, America's largest homebuilder, said it planned to reduce the size of its homes \"to address affordability for homebuyers.\" In a July earnings call, the company's chief operating officer, Michael Murray, said D.R. Horton's average house size was down 2% compared to last year.\n\nThere are signs those efforts might be helping buyers get in the door: The median sales price of existing homes jumped to $426,900 in June, according to the National Association of Realtors, while the median price of new homes in June was $417,300, according to the US Census Bureau.\n\nSmaller homes and fewer amenities\n\nHeather Eisenmann, a Realtor who specializes in selling new construction in Clarksville, Tennessee, said that she's noticed that many of the new homes she shows now have smaller bedrooms and living rooms. Many no longer have freestanding tubs to save on bathroom space, and formal dining rooms are often swapped out for kitchen islands built for bar seating, she said.\n\n\"Three years ago, we were building in a subdivision called Easthaven. Those homes were 2,500 to 3,200 square feet,\" she said. \"Everything I put on today is under 2,000.\"\n\nEisenmann said she's also recently noticed that some homebuilders are skipping expensive appliances and \"extras\" like fences and window treatments to lower a home's list price, a strategy she thinks could backfire and repel first-time buyers.\n\n\"New construction is appealing to a first-time homebuyer because it requires little to no maintenance,\" she said. \"But when they start looking at new homes that don't come with all the appliances, most of my first-time homebuyers want to turn to existing homes.\"\n\nCarias, from Muscatine, said she's happy with her new under-1,000-square-foot home and doesn't have plans to move anytime soon. She and her boyfriend relocated from the Sacramento area back to her hometown to find a home within their price range.\n\n\"Our biggest goal was getting to carve out a tiny little slice of something that is ours and not throw away our money on renting anymore,\" she said. \"I am so excited to paint and do whatever I want.\"\n\nU.S. Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Trends in U.S. Housing Inventory Large Cities With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory States With the Biggest Increase in Housing Inventory Methodology State of the housing industry Residential Housing Inventory by State Changes in Housing Inventory by State\n\nThe-CNN-Wire\u2122 & \u00a9 2024 Cable News Network, Inc., a Warner Bros.
